reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import math

import torch
import torch.nn as nn


class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Causal grouped-query attention with KV-head expansion.

    Query heads outnumber KV heads. Keys and values are expanded by repeating
    each KV head across its assigned query-head group before causal attention.
    """

    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()

    def forward(
        self,
        q: torch.Tensor,
        k: torch.Tensor,
        v: torch.Tensor,
    ) -> torch.Tensor:
        batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, head_dim = q.shape
        num_kv_heads = k.shape[2]
        group_size = num_q_heads // num_kv_heads

        expanded_k = k.repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2)
        expanded_v = v.repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2)

        out = torch.zeros_like(q)
        scale = 1.0 / math.sqrt(head_dim)

        for b in range(batch_size):
            for h in range(num_q_heads):
                for t in range(seq_len):
                    q_t = q[b, t, h]
                    k_hist = expanded_k[b, : t + 1, h]
                    v_hist = expanded_v[b, : t + 1, h]
                    scores = (k_hist * q_t.unsqueeze(0)).sum(dim=-1) * scale
                    attn = torch.softmax(scores, dim=0)
                    out[b, t, h] = (attn.unsqueeze(-1) * v_hist).sum(dim=0)

        return out


batch_size = 2
seq_len = 80
num_q_heads = 8
num_kv_heads = 2
head_dim = 32


def get_inputs():
    q = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    k = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_kv_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    v = torch.randn(batch_size, seq_len, num_kv_heads, head_dim, dtype=torch.float32)
    return [q, k, v]


def get_init_inputs():
    return []

Let's inspect the reference. It computes causal attention: for each batch b, each query head h, each time step t (0-indexed), it uses q[b, t, h] and keys/values from expansion up to t (i.e., positions 0..t inclusive). So it's a causal attention (autoregressive). q shape: (batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, head_dim). k, v shape: (batch_size, seq_len, num_kv_heads, head_dim). group_size = num_q_heads // num_kv_heads. expanded_k = k.repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2). So each KV head is repeated group_size times to match num_q_heads. Then for each b, h, t: - q_t shape (head_dim) - k_hist shape (t+1, head_dim) - v_hist shape (t+1, head_dim) - scores = (k_hist * q_t.unsqueeze(0)).sum(dim=-1) * scale => dot product of q with each key, scaled. - attn = softmax(scores, dim=0) over the t+1 positions. - out = sum over positions of attn_i * v_i. This is standard causal attention but with grouped query heads. Since seq_len is only 80, batch 2, heads 8, head_dim 32. Very small. Online softmax algorithm (used in FlashAttention): - Initialize m = -inf, l = 0, acc = 0 (vector of head_dim for output). - For j = 0..t: - score = dot(q, k_j) * scale - m_new = max(m, score) - exp_factor = exp(m - m_new) // if m == -inf, factor = 0? In forward: - Determine device = q.device - Move q, k, v to CUDA (if not already): q_cuda = q.cuda(), etc. - Allocate out_cuda = torch.zeros_like(q_cuda) - Call the CUDA kernel (via the loaded extension) - Move out back: out = out_cuda.to(device) - Return out Need to handle if CUDA is not available? Now, writing the C++ wrapper: The wrapper function signature should be something like: torch::Tensor causal_gqa_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v); Inside: - auto batch_size = q.size(0); - auto seq_len = q.size(1); - auto num_q_heads = q.size(2); - auto head_dim = q.size(3); - auto num_kv_heads = k.size(2); - auto group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; - check that q, k, v are contiguous, float32, on CUDA. - allocate out = torch::zeros_like(q); - define total_threads = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; - block_size = 128; // maybe 256 - num_blocks = (total_threads + block_size - 1) / block_size; - launch kernel. - return out. The kernel itself: __global__ void causal_gqa_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, float scale) { int tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; if (tid >= total) return; // decode indices int tmp = tid; int t = tmp % seq_len; tmp /= seq_len; int h = tmp % num_q_heads; int b = tmp / num_q_heads; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int kv_head = h / group_size; // load q const float* q_ptr = q + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; float q_val[32]; // assume max head_dim 32? // To be generic, we can use a loop. But local array size needs to be constant. // Since head_dim is variable, we cannot use VLA. But we know head_dim is 32. // If we want to be generic, we can allocate a local array of size up to a max, e.g., 128. // Let's just use a loop to load into a register if possible, but we need a temporary store. // Since head_dim is small, we can load it into an array declared with maximum size. } Actually, C++ doesn't support VLAs. But we can use a fixed maximum size, say 128 or 256, and loop up to head_dim. The compiler will allocate that fixed-size array in local memory (or registers if optimized). Since head_dim is passed as a runtime value, the compiler cannot guarantee it's <= max_size, but we can add an assert. Alternatively, we can load q elements on the fly inside the j loops: for each j, we loop over d and read q[d] directly. That avoids a local array for q. But we'd read q[d] repeatedly for each j. q is in global memory but cached. Since D=32 and j up to 80, q is read 80 times. That's okay. But we can keep it in registers by using a small fixed array. Let's declare `float q_val[128];` and then `for (int d=0; d<head_dim; ++d) q_val[d] = q_ptr[d];`. This is safe as long as head_dim <= 128. The problem has head_dim=32. Similarly, `float out_acc[128];` for accumulation. For the scores, we recompute k each pass. Let's write the kernel: __global__ void causal_gqa_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, float scale) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; if (idx >= total) return; int t = idx % seq_len; int tmp = idx / seq_len; int h = tmp % num_q_heads; int b = tmp / num_q_heads; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int kv_head = h / group_size; const float* q_ptr = q + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; float qv[128]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { qv[d] = q_ptr[d]; } // compute max score float max_score = -1e30f; for (int j = 0; j <= t; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { s += qv[d] * k_ptr[d]; } s *= scale; if (s > max_score) max_score = s; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; float acc[128]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) acc[d] = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= t; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = k + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { s += qv[d] * k_ptr[d]; } s *= scale; float e = expf(s - max_score); sum_exp += e; const float* v_ptr = v + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { acc[d] += e * v_ptr[d]; } } float inv = 1.0f / sum_exp; float* out_ptr = out + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < head_dim; ++d) { out_ptr[d] = acc[d] * inv; } } Potential issue: If head_dim > 128, out-of-bounds. But head_dim is 32. Fine. Now, the host wrapper: torch::Tensor causal_gqa_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { // checks TORCH_CHECK(q.is_cuda(), "q must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(k.is_cuda(), "k must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(v.is_cuda(), "v must be CUDA"); TORCH_CHECK(q.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "q must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(k.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "k must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(v.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "v must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(q.is_contiguous() && k.is_contiguous() && v.is_contiguous(), "tensors must be contiguous"); int batch_size = q.size(0); int seq_len = q.size(1); int num_q_heads = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); int num_kv_heads = k.size(2); TORCH_CHECK(num_q_heads % num_kv_heads == 0, "num_q_heads must be divisible by num_kv_heads"); TORCH_CHECK(k.size(0) == batch_size && k.size(1) == seq_len && k.size(3) == head_dim, "k shape mismatch"); TORCH_CHECK(v.size(0) == batch_size && v.size(1) == seq_len && v.size(2) == num_kv_heads && v.size(3) == head_dim, "v shape mismatch"); auto out = torch::zeros_like(q); int total_threads = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; const int block_size = 128; int grid_size = (total_threads + block_size - 1) / block_size; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf((float)head_dim); causal_gqa_forward_kernel<<<grid_size, block_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, num_kv_heads, head_dim, scale); C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK(); return out; } But `TORCH_CHECK` and `C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK` are available in libtorch headers when using `cuda_sources`. Wait: `C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK()` checks that `cudaGetLastError()` is `cudaSuccess` before the launch (to clear previous errors), validates grid/block dimensions against device limits, and then after the launch checks for launch errors. If q is on CPU, it moves to CUDA. `.to(device)` moves back to CPU. Good. But what if the input is on CPU but half precision? The reference uses float32. We call `.float()`, so it's okay. But the output should match the reference's dtype? The reference's output dtype is float32 because input is float32. So fine. Potential issue: moving to CUDA and back introduces a small numerical difference? The computation is done in fp32 on CUDA, same as CPU reference. The reference uses PyTorch's GPU? Actually reference runs on CPU with Python loops and PyTorch operations. PyTorch CPU softmax uses the same algorithm. Our kernel should produce the same within tolerance. But there could be a slight difference because of the order of summation. The CPU reference does: scores = (k_hist * q_t.unsqueeze(0)).sum(dim=-1) * scale This is a batched matrix-vector product. The sum is done in PyTorch's CPU implementation, which likely uses vectorized accumulation order. Our kernel does a simple loop with accumulators. The difference should be within 1e-4 for fp32. However, there is a subtle point: The reference computes `expanded_k` and `expanded_v` by `repeat_interleave`. This changes memory layout: `expanded_k` has shape (B, T, Hq, D) and is contiguous in memory (since repeat_interleave creates a new contiguous tensor). Then `k_hist = expanded_k[b, : t + 1, h]` slices along T and Hq. The CPU reference then does `(k_hist * q_t.unsqueeze(0)).sum(dim=-1)`. This computes dot products for each of the t+1 positions. Our kernel reads directly from `k` with `kv_head = h // group_size`. The values are the same, but we need to be careful: `repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2)` replicates each kv head `group_size` times consecutively. So the mapping `kv_head = h // group_size` is correct. For example, Hq=8, Hkv=2, group_size=4. Heads: h=0,1,2,3 -> kv_head=0; h=4,5,6,7 -> kv_head=1. That's correct. One more nuance: The reference does `torch.softmax(scores, dim=0)`. `scores` is a 1D tensor of length t+1. PyTorch's softmax subtracts the max and divides by sum. Our implementation does exactly that. But does PyTorch handle `-inf` for empty sequences? Here t ranges 0..seq_len-1, so always at least one element. Another nuance: We need to make sure our kernel `expf` uses fast math maybe, which can have slightly lower precision. The `--use_fast_math` flag enables faster but less accurate math. The tolerance is 1e-4, which should be fine with fast math. However, to be safe, maybe remove `--use_fast_math`. Let's keep `-O3` and maybe `--use_fast_math` is okay. But let's not risk it. I'll use just `["-O3"]`. Also, the `C10_CUDA_KERNEL_LAUNCH_CHECK()` macro checks CUDA errors. But it might not be available in some PyTorch versions. A safer alternative is: ``` AT_CUDA_CHECK(cudaGetLastError()); ``` But `AT_CUDA_CHECK` requires `<ATen/cuda/CUDAContext.h>`. `<torch/extension.h>` might not include it. Alternatively: ``` cudaError_t err = cudaGetLastError(); if (err != cudaSuccess) { AT_ERROR("CUDA kernel launch failed: ", cudaGetErrorString(err)); } ``` But `AT_ERROR` is deprecated. Better to use `TORCH_CHECK`: ``` TORCH_CHECK(cudaGetLastError() == cudaSuccess, "CUDA kernel launch failed: ", cudaGetErrorString(cudaGetLastError())); ``` But note that calling cudaGetLastError twice: first to get error, second for string. Let's do: ``` cudaError_t err = cudaGetLastError(); if (err != cudaSuccess) { TORCH_CHECK(false, "CUDA kernel failed: ", cudaGetErrorString(err)); } ``` Actually, launch errors are synchronous. We'll use that. Let's also consider using `constexpr int MAX_HEAD_DIM = 128;` and `float qv[MAX_HEAD_DIM];` etc. That's fine. Now, let's write the full kernel code and compile it. We'll test compile, correctness, and then submit. One more thing: `load_inline` caches the compiled extension based on source strings and flags. Changing the source will recompile. Even better: we can compute the dot product in a single pass using the online softmax algorithm (from FlashAttention). Let's see: - We need max score and weighted sum of v. - We can do it in one pass using running max and rescaling. Algorithm: Initialize `m = -inf`, `l = 0`, `acc[d] = 0`. For j = 0..t: s_j = scale * dot(q, k_j) m_new = max(m, s_j) P_j = exp(s_j - m_new) l = l * exp(m - m_new) + P_j acc = acc * exp(m - m_new) + P_j * v_j m = m_new At the end, out = acc / l. This allows a single pass over j, halving the K/V memory reads. For small head_dim, this is a nice improvement. But the two-pass approach might already be fast enough. However, with a single pass we don't need to store q or acc in arrays across passes; we can accumulate `acc[d]` in registers with a fixed unroll. Wait, acc[d] still needs storage for head_dim floats. Template kernel: ```cpp template <int HEAD_DIM> __global__ void causal_gqa_forward_kernel(...) { ... float qv[HEAD_DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int d=0; d<HEAD_DIM; ++d) qv[d] = q_ptr[d]; float max_score = -1e30f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int j=0; j<=t; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = ...; #pragma unroll for (int d=0; d<HEAD_DIM; ++d) s += qv[d] * k_ptr[d]; s *= scale; if (s > max_score) max_score = s; } float sum_exp = 0.0f; float acc[HEAD_DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int d=0; d<HEAD_DIM; ++d) acc[d] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int j=0; j<=t; ++j) { float s = 0.0f; const float* k_ptr = ...; #pragma unroll for (int d=0; d<HEAD_DIM; ++d) s += qv[d] * k_ptr[d]; s *= scale; float e = expf(s - max_score); sum_exp += e; const float* v_ptr = ...; #pragma unroll for (int d=0; d<HEAD_DIM; ++d) acc[d] += e * v_ptr[d]; } float inv_sum = 1.0f / sum_exp; float* out_ptr = out + ... #pragma unroll for (int d=0; d<HEAD_DIM; ++d) out_ptr[d] = acc[d] * inv_sum; } ``` And host function uses switch/case for supported head_dims. But for correctness, I need to make sure the numerical result matches. The two-pass softmax is more numerically stable but the online version is also stable and mathematically equivalent. However, with floating point, they might differ slightly but should be within tolerance (1e-4). The online softmax algorithm: Initialize: m_0 = -inf l_0 = 0 acc[d] = 0 For j=0..t: s_j = scale * dot(q, k_j) m_new = max(m, s_j) P_j = exp(s_j - m_new) l = l * exp(m - m_new) + P_j for d: acc[d] = acc[d] * exp(m - m_new) + P_j * v_j[d] m = m_new After loop, for d: out[d] = acc[d] / l Is this equivalent to the reference? Yes, mathematically. Let's check with a small example to be safe. But this is the standard online softmax. However, there is a subtlety: what if all s_j are very negative and exp(s_j - m_new) is 0 for some? Should be fine. Single pass is better because it reads K and V only once. For each thread, total K reads: (t+1)*D. Total V reads: (t+1)*D. Two pass would be 2*(t+1)*D for K and (t+1)*D for V. So single pass saves about 33% memory bandwidth. Also, we avoid storing q in registers across two loops. We read q each time. Or we can still store it. But since K is read once, q can be stored. Let's try the single-pass approach. One concern: the compiler might not be able to keep `acc[d]` in registers if we index with a variable `d` in a loop. Another idea: use `float2` or `float4` loads for head_dim to boost memory throughput. Since head_dim is 32 (a multiple of 4 and 8), we can use `float4`. This is a good optimization. Using `float4` for Q, K, V access: - K row: 32 floats -> 8 float4s. - V row: same. - Q row: same. We can write template loops that step by 4 or 8. But to keep it simple and correct, `float4` access is easy. ```cpp template <int HEAD_DIM> __global__ void kernel(...) { // ... float qv[HEAD_DIM]; const float4* q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM/4; ++d) { float4 tmp = q4[d]; qv[d*4+0] = tmp.x; qv[d*4+1] = tmp.y; qv[d*4+2] = tmp.z; qv[d*4+3] = tmp.w; } // ... } ``` But this assumes head_dim is divisible by 4. It is 32. Good. This uses vectorized loads. But the tensor pointers might not be 16-byte aligned? PyTorch allocates tensors with alignment >= 256 bytes typically, so row starts are 16-byte aligned. Yes. However, the indexing for K and V involves `((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim`. Since `head_dim` is 32, and the base pointer is aligned, each row start is aligned to `32*4 = 128` bytes, which is divisible by 16. So `float4` loads are safe and aligned. Let's implement vectorized loads using `float4`. This should significantly improve memory bandwidth. Actually, for Q we can also use `float4`. But writing this for a template might be verbose but straightforward. Let me draft the kernel: ```cpp template <int HEAD_DIM> __global__ void causal_gqa_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, float scale) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; if (idx >= total) return; int t = idx % seq_len; int tmp = idx / seq_len; int h = tmp % num_q_heads; int b = tmp / num_q_heads; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int kv_head = h / group_size; const float* q_ptr = q + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; float qv[HEAD_DIM]; // Load Q with float4 const float4* q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM / 4; ++d) { float4 tmp4 = q4[d]; qv[d*4+0] = tmp4.x; qv[d*4+1] = tmp4.y; qv[d*4+2] = tmp4.z; qv[d*4+3] = tmp4.w; } float m = -1e30f; float l = 0.0f; float acc[HEAD_DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM; ++d) acc[d] = 0.0f; #pragma unroll 4 for (int j = 0; j <= t; ++j) { const float* k_ptr = k + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; float s = 0.0f; const float4* k4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM / 4; ++d) { float4 tmp4 = k4[d]; s += qv[d*4+0] * tmp4.x; s += qv[d*4+1] * tmp4.y; s += qv[d*4+2] * tmp4.z; s += qv[d*4+3] * tmp4.w; } s *= scale; float m_new = fmaxf(m, s); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); // = exp(old_m - new_m), if new_m > old_m, this < 1 float exp_s = expf(s - m_new); // if new_m == s, this is 1 l = l * exp_m_diff + exp_s; const float* v_ptr = v + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; const float4* v4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM / 4; ++d) { float4 tmp4 = v4[d]; acc[d*4+0] = acc[d*4+0] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.x; acc[d*4+1] = acc[d*4+1] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.y; acc[d*4+2] = acc[d*4+2] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.z; acc[d*4+3] = acc[d*4+3] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.w; } m = m_new; } float inv_l = 1.0f / l; float* out_ptr = out + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; float4* out4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(out_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM / 4; ++d) { float4 tmp4; tmp4.x = acc[d*4+0] * inv_l; tmp4.y = acc[d*4+1] * inv_l; tmp4.z = acc[d*4+2] * inv_l; tmp4.w = acc[d*4+3] * inv_l; out4[d] = tmp4; } } ``` Wait, does this compute the exact same as two-pass softmax? Let's verify. Two-pass: max = max_j s_j sum_exp = sum_j exp(s_j - max) acc = sum_j exp(s_j - max) * v_j out = acc / sum_exp Online: m_0 = -inf, l_0 = 0, acc_0 = 0. At step j: m_{j+1} = max(m_j, s_j) P_j = exp(s_j - m_{j+1}) l_{j+1} = l_j * exp(m_j - m_{j+1}) + P_j acc_{j+1} = acc_j * exp(m_j - m_{j+1}) + P_j * v_j After all steps, acc_final = sum_j exp(s_j - m_final) * v_j, l_final = sum_j exp(s_j - m_final). out = acc_final / l_final. This is correct. Need to be careful about `m` initial value. If t < 0 (i.e., no iterations), but t ranges from 0 to seq_len-1, so at least j=0 is executed. So m starts at -inf, then after j=0, m becomes s_0. l becomes 1.0 * exp(-inf - s_0)? Wait: Initially m = -inf, l = 0. j=0: m_new = max(-inf, s_0) = s_0. exp_m_diff = exp(-inf - s_0) = 0. exp_s = exp(s_0 - s_0) = 1. l = 0 * 0 + 1 = 1. acc = 0 * 0 + 1 * v_0 = v_0. This is correct: after first step, output is v_0. Great. But will this be numerically equivalent to reference's softmax within 1e-4? The reference computes softmax in float32 with two passes (max then exp and sum). The online algorithm does one pass with rescaling, but intermediate accumulations differ in order. Order of additions changes results slightly (Kahan summation vs naive). Because t is up to 80, the difference should be small, well within 1e-4. Let's verify with a small example mentally: s = [1.0, 2.0] Reference: max = 2.0 exp = [exp(-1), exp(0)] = [0.3679, 1.0] sum = 1.3679 out = (0.3679*v0 + 1.0*v1) / 1.3679 Online: j=0: m=1.0, l=1.0, acc=v0 j=1: m_new=2.0, exp_m_diff=exp(-1)=0.3679, exp_s=1.0 l = 1.0*0.3679 + 1.0 = 1.3679 acc = v0*0.3679 + v1*1.0 out = acc / 1.3679 Same. For more elements, the property holds exactly in real arithmetic. In floating point, the operations are: `acc_new = acc_old * factor + P_j * v_j` The order of operations is slightly different than summing all `exp(s_j - max) * v_j` then dividing. But since there are only 80 terms max, the rounding error should be small (<1e-6 relative). Tolerance is 1e-4. So it's fine. However, there is one issue: `exp_m_diff` can underflow to 0 if `m - m_new` is very negative. If `m` was say 0 and `m_new` is 100, `exp(-100) = 3.7e-44`. In fp32, this underflows to 0. That's okay because the old accumulator's contribution is negligible. The new `m` is accurate. The sum `l` will be dominated by the new term. This is exactly why the online softmax is stable. But does it match the reference's two-pass computation? Reference computes `exp(s_j - max)` where max is the true max. The online algorithm effectively computes the same, but if `m` was updated by a huge jump, the old partial sums are scaled by exp(-100)=0 and lost. The reference would compute those old terms directly as exp(s_old - max_final) which might also underflow to 0. So they match. Good. Given the tiny problem size, the simple approach should be enough. However, there is an alternative: use PyTorch native operations to vectorize the reference. For example: ```python expanded_k = k.repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2) expanded_v = v.repeat_interleave(group_size, dim=2) scores = torch.einsum('bsqh,bssh->bqht'?) No, that's not causal. ``` Actually, vectorizing causal attention can be done with `torch.matmul` and masking: ```python scores = torch.einsum('bqhd,bkhd->bqhk', q, expanded_k) * scale mask = torch.triu(torch.ones(seq_len, seq_len, device=q.device, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1) scores = scores.masked_fill(mask, float('-inf')) attn = torch.softmax(scores, dim=-1) out = torch.einsum('bqhk,bkhd->bqhd', attn, expanded_v) ``` BUT the shape is (batch, seq_len, num_q_heads, head_dim). `torch.einsum('bqhd,bkhd->bqhk', q, expanded_k)` does: - q: (B, Q, H, D) - k: (B, K, H, D) - output: (B, Q, H, K) if we map `b q h d, b k h d -> b q h k`. Wait, `torch.einsum` with indices 'bqhd,bkhd->bqhk': q is b,q,h,d; k is b,k,h,d; result is b,q,h,k. Yes. But `q` indices: batch, seq_len (t), num_q_heads (h), head_dim (d). `k` indices: batch, seq_len (j), num_q_heads (h), head_dim (d). So the result is (batch, seq_len_q, num_q_heads, seq_len_k). Then we apply causal mask and softmax over the last dim (seq_len_k). Then einsum with v: (batch, seq_len, num_q_heads, seq_len) @ (batch, seq_len, num_q_heads, head_dim) -> (batch, seq_len_q, num_q_heads, head_dim). This is mathematically equivalent and runs as a few large matmuls on GPU. It would be extremely fast. But the task explicitly says "Replace the forward computation ... with a custom CUDA kernel". It wants a custom CUDA kernel. If we use einsum, it wouldn't be a custom CUDA kernel. So we must write a CUDA extension. But wait, could the reference on GPU (with CUDA tensors) already be slow because of Python loops but still run on GPU via tiny kernels? Yes. Our custom kernel should definitely beat that. One potential issue: the online softmax with `float4` might not compile if `reinterpret_cast` is used on `__restrict__` pointers? No, it's fine. But I need to make sure the pointer is aligned. Usually `float4` requires 16-byte alignment. As I noted, PyTorch tensors are aligned to at least 256 bytes. So the row start is aligned. But what if `head_dim` is 32 and `num_q_heads` is 8? The row stride in q for different t is `num_q_heads * head_dim * sizeof(float)` = 8*32*4 = 1024 bytes. So `q_ptr` is aligned to 1024, which is divisible by 16. Good. For k, row stride is `num_kv_heads * head_dim * sizeof(float)` = 2*32*4 = 256 bytes, divisible by 16. So `float4` loads are aligned. Let's write the code now. Actually, one more thought: should I worry about `expf` vs `exp`? In CUDA, `expf` is for float. Use `expf`. For `fmaxf`, use `fmaxf`. Good. Also, should I worry about the `scale` computation? `1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(head_dim))`. Correct. Let me write the Python code. ```python import math import time import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline CUDA_SOURCE = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cmath> template <int HEAD_DIM> __global__ void causal_gqa_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, float scale) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; if (idx >= total) return; int t = idx % seq_len; int tmp = idx / seq_len; int h = tmp % num_q_heads; int b = tmp / num_q_heads; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int kv_head = h / group_size; const float* q_ptr = q + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; float qv[HEAD_DIM]; const float4* q4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(q_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM / 4; ++d) { float4 tmp4 = q4[d]; qv[d*4+0] = tmp4.x; qv[d*4+1] = tmp4.y; qv[d*4+2] = tmp4.z; qv[d*4+3] = tmp4.w; } float m = -1e30f; float l = 0.0f; float acc[HEAD_DIM]; #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM; ++d) { acc[d] = 0.0f; } for (int j = 0; j <= t; ++j) { const float* k_ptr = k + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; float s = 0.0f; const float4* k4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(k_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM / 4; ++d) { float4 tmp4 = k4[d]; s += qv[d*4+0] * tmp4.x; s += qv[d*4+1] * tmp4.y; s += qv[d*4+2] * tmp4.z; s += qv[d*4+3] * tmp4.w; } s *= scale; float m_new = fmaxf(m, s); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_s = expf(s - m_new); l = l * exp_m_diff + exp_s; const float* v_ptr = v + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; const float4* v4 = reinterpret_cast<const float4*>(v_ptr); #pragma unroll for (int d = 0; d < HEAD_DIM / 4; ++d) { float4 tmp4 = v4[d]; acc[d*4+0] = acc[d*4+0] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.x; acc[d*4+1] = acc[d*4+1] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.y; acc[d*4+2] = acc[d*4+2] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.z; acc[d*4+3] = acc[d*4+3] * exp_m_diff + exp_s * tmp4.w; } m = m_new; } float inv_l = 1.0f / l; float* out_ptr = out + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; float4* out4 = reinterpret_cast<float4*>(out_ptr); } } torch::Tensor causal_gqa_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { TORCH_CHECK(q.is_cuda(), "q must be a CUDA tensor"); TORCH_CHECK(k.is_cuda(), "k must be a CUDA tensor"); TORCH_CHECK(v.is_cuda(), "v must be a CUDA tensor"); TORCH_CHECK(q.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "q must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(k.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "k must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(v.dtype() == torch::kFloat32, "v must be float32"); TORCH_CHECK(q.is_contiguous() && k.is_contiguous() && v.is_contiguous(), "inputs must be contiguous"); int batch_size = q.size(0); int seq_len = q.size(1); int num_q_heads = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); int num_kv_heads = k.size(2); TORCH_CHECK(num_q_heads % num_kv_heads == 0, "num_q_heads must be divisible by num_kv_heads"); TORCH_CHECK(k.size(0) == batch_size && k.size(1) == seq_len && k.size(3) == head_dim, "k shape mismatch"); TORCH_CHECK(v.size(0) == batch_size && v.size(1) == seq_len && v.size(2) == num_kv_heads && v.size(3) == head_dim, "v shape mismatch"); switch (head_dim) { case 16: causal_gqa_forward_kernel<16><<<grid_size, block_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, num_kv_heads, head_dim, scale); Also, `exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new)` when m == m_new (i.e. s_j <= m). In that case `exp(0) = 1`. But `m - m_new` can be 0 exactly if equal or negative. If m_new = m (i.e. current s_j is not the new max), then `exp_m_diff = expf(0) = 1`. `exp_s = expf(s_j - m)`. This is correct. Do we risk `m` staying at -inf if t < 0? No, t >= 0. So fine. Wait, PyTorch default layout is `(batch, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim)`, with `head_dim` being the last dimension, so elements within a head are contiguous. The stride for heads is head_dim, and for seq_len is num_heads * head_dim. So to read `q_ptr + d` for `d in 0..31`, it's contiguous. This is very elegant and efficient. Each block loads its own query and accumulates. Memory loads for `k` and `v` are coalesced because adjacent threads (`d`) load adjacent memory for a given `j`. Kernel code: ```cuda __global__ void causal_gqa_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, float scale) { extern __shared__ float sdata[]; // size = blockDim.x * sizeof(float) int total_blocks = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; int block_id = blockIdx.x; if (block_id >= total_blocks) return; int t = block_id % seq_len; int tmp = block_id / seq_len; int h = tmp % num_q_heads; int b = tmp / num_q_heads; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int kv_head = h / group_size; int d = threadIdx.x; if (d >= head_dim) return; // but block_dim == head_dim, so this may be unnecessary const float* q_ptr = q + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; float qd = q_ptr[d]; float acc = 0.0f; float m = -1e30f; float l = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= t; ++j) { const float* k_ptr = k + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; float kd = k_ptr[d]; // compute dot product via shared memory reduction sdata[d] = qd * kd; __syncthreads(); for (int offset = blockDim.x / 2; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { if (d < offset) { sdata[d] += sdata[d + offset]; } __syncthreads(); } float score = sdata[0] * scale; // online softmax update float m_new = fmaxf(m, score); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_s = expf(score - m_new); l = l * exp_m_diff + exp_s; const float* v_ptr = v + ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim; float vd = v_ptr[d]; acc = acc * exp_m_diff + exp_s * vd; m = m_new; } float inv_l = 1.0f / l; float* out_ptr = out + ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim; out_ptr[d] = acc * inv_l; } ``` Wait, there's a subtle issue: the shared memory reduction writes `sdata[d]` and then reduces. But what if `head_dim` is not a power of two? The reduction loop `offset = blockDim.x / 2` assumes it. If `head_dim=32` (power of two), it's fine. Actually a robust reduction: ``` int tid = threadIdx.x; sdata[tid] = qd * kd; __syncthreads(); for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1) { if (tid < stride && tid + stride < blockDim.x) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + stride]; } __syncthreads(); } float score = sdata[0] * scale; ``` For `blockDim.x = 24`: - stride=12: tid<12 and tid<12? Actually `tid+12 < 24` is always true for tid<12. So threads 0..11 sum with 12..23. OK. - stride=6: tid<6 and tid+6<24 true. Sum 0..5 with 6..11. OK. - stride=3: tid<3 and tid+3<24. Sum 0..2 with 3..5. OK. - stride=1: tid<1 and tid+1<24. Thread 0 sums `sdata[0] += sdata[1]`. But `sdata[2]` hasn't been added to `sdata[0]` or `sdata[1]`. So it's missing `sdata[2]`. Thus this doesn't work for non-power-of-two. To make it robust, we can just loop `for (int stride = blockDim.x / 2; stride > 0; stride >>= 1)` but after each step, we need the active threads to be contiguous starting from 0, and the elements they sum should be also contiguous. For 24: - stride=12: active 0..11, sum with 12..23. After this, sum is in 0..11. - stride=6: active 0..5, sum with 6..11. After this, sum is in 0..5. - stride=3: active 0..2, sum with 3..5. After this, sum is in 0..2. - stride=1: active 0..0? If we use `if (tid < stride)`, only thread 0 is active. It sums 0 and 1. Sum of 0..2 is missing 2. The issue is that 3 is odd. So for non-power-of-two, the active threads at the last step don't cover the remaining elements. A common fix is to use a tree reduction where the number of active threads is `ceil(active/2)`. But implementing that in a simple loop: ``` int active = blockDim.x; for (int stride = (active + 1) / 2; stride > 0; stride = (active + 1) / 2) { if (tid < stride && tid + stride < active) { sdata[tid] += sdata[tid + stride]; } active = stride; __syncthreads(); } ``` Wait, that's also tricky because `active` changes and threads may diverge? Actually all threads hit `__syncthreads` if they reach it. The `if` guard ensures some threads don't write, but all threads must reach the `__syncthreads`. This is okay as long as the `if` doesn't contain `return` or break before `__syncthreads`. Let's test `active = 24`: - stride = (24+1)/2 = 12. tid<12 and tid+12<24: true. sdata[0..11] += sdata[12..23]. active becomes 12. - stride = (12+1)/2 = 6. tid<6 and tid+6<12: true. sdata[0..5] += sdata[6..11]. active becomes 6. - stride = (6+1)/2 = 3. tid<3 and tid+3<6: true. sdata[0..2] += sdata[3..5]. active becomes 3. - stride = (3+1)/2 = 2. tid<2 and tid+2<3: tid=0 -> 0+2<3 true, sdata[0] += sdata[2]. tid=1 -> 1+2<3 false. active becomes 2. - stride = (2+1)/2 = 1. tid<1 and tid+1<2: tid=0 -> true, sdata[0] += sdata[1]. active becomes 1. - stride = (1+1)/2 = 1. tid<1 and tid+1<1: false. active becomes 1 (or 0?). Loop ends when stride == 0? If we have condition `stride > 0`, it would continue. We need to break when active == 1. The profiling tool isn't available. Let me optimize further based on what I know about the kernel. The current kernel uses 1 thread per (batch, head, time) and has O(seq_len^2) work per thread. Since seq_len=80 is small but num_q_heads * batch_size = 2*8 = 16 total tokens (actually 2*8*80 = 1280 total positions), and each position must loop over up to 80 kv positions, the kernel is doing 1280 * 80 = 102,400 dot products of length 32. The problem is that each thread does a lot of sequential work (up to 80 iterations over kv positions) and each iteration accesses a different location in kv cache. This means memory reads aren't coalesced well. Let me think of a better approach. Instead of one thread per position, I could have multiple threads per position (e.g. 32 threads per position, one for each head dimension element). Or I could have each block handle one (batch, head) and process all seq_len positions in parallel using a shared memory approach. For a highly efficient approach, let me think about using shared memory. Option: one block per (batch, q_head, or block of q_heads) - Load q into registers (or smem) - Load kv into shared memory tile-by-tile - Compute dot products using shared memory But since seq_len=80 and head_dim=32 the kv cache is only 80*32*4 = 10KB per kv head. This is tiny and fits in L1. Actually, the main issue with the current kernel is that k and v accesses have poor locality and are not coalesced across threads. Since each thread reads from a different kv_head history, adjacent threads access different memory locations. A better approach: process the same (batch, kv_head) with many threads working on different q positions, or group positions. Actually, a really good approach for small seq_len would be to have each warp (32 threads) handle a single (b, q_head, t) and use warp-level parallelism for the dot product. But that would mean we need 32x more blocks. Wait, the current issue is memory access pattern: - Thread 0: accesses k[b, 0, kv_head0, :] then k[b, 1, kv_head0, :]... per step. - Thread 1: accesses k[b, 0, kv_head1, :] then k[b, 1, kv_head1, :]... So adjacent threads touch different kv_heads - their accesses are not coalesced. If I organize so adjacent threads work on the same (b, h, j) accessing different dimensions d, then memory accesses would be coalesced! Let me try: one thread per (b, q_head, t, d) - so 32 threads per (b, h, t) work together on a single q position. Then for each kv position j from 0 to t: 1. Thread d computes s_d = q[b,t,h,d] * k[b,j,kv_head,d] 2. Warp-level reduce sum to get s = sum(s_d) 3. Broadcast s to all threads 4. Thread d computes e * v[b,j,kv_head,d] and accumulates. 5. After loop, normalize. This way k and v accesses for a given j across a warp are fully coalesced since thread d reads dimension d. The total grid size would be batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len * (head_dim / 32) since 32 threads = 1 warp. But we need each warp to output a full vector of head_dim. Wait, what we want is: - Each warp (32 threads) computes one output position (b, h, t) - Thread d within the warp computes dimension d The algorithm per warp: - Load q_t[d] for each thread d (can be loaded from global memory, each thread reads its own dimension - coalesced if threads are arranged right) - Initialize max_score = -inf (warp-level shared, store in register) - Initialize sum_exp = 0 and o_acc[d] = 0 for each thread - For j from 0 to t: - Thread d computes partial = q_t[d] * k[b,j,kv_head,d] - Warp reduce sum across all d → s_j - Thread d updates o_acc[d] += exp(s_j - max_score) * v[b,j,kv_head,d] and sum_exp += exp(s_j - max_score) (but need to handle max_score updates carefully) Actually for numerical stability we should use the online softmax approach: - Let m_{i} = max of scores up to i - Let l_{i} = sum of exp(scores - m_{i}) up to i - Update formulas: - m_new = max(m, s) - l_new = l * exp(m - m_new) + exp(s - m_new) - o_new = o * exp(m - m_new) + v * exp(s - m_new) Let's implement this with warp primitives. Each thread d maintains o_acc[d], and we maintain a shared m and l across the warp. The warp reduce can be done using __shfl_sync. Let me write the kernel: ```cuda // One warp per (b, h, t) // ThreadIdx.x is split: warp_id and lane_id // lane_id = threadIdx.x % 32, warp_id = threadIdx.x / 32 // Actually let's think about block organization: // blockDim.x = 128 (4 warps) // Each warp computes one (b, h, t) // So each block computes 4 (b, h, t)'s // grid size = (total_positions + 3) / 4 // For each warp: __shared__ float smem_q[4][32]; // 4 warps * 32 dims // Actually let's just use registers ``` Let me write a cleaner version: ```cpp __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } return val; } __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_max(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset /= 2) { val = fmaxf(val, __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset)); } return val; } __global__ void causal_gqa_forward_kernel(...) { int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; // threadIdx.x / 32 int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31; // threadIdx.x % 32 int warps_per_block = blockDim.x >> 5; int global_warp_id = blockIdx.x * warps_per_block + warp_id; int total_warps = (batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len); if (global_warp_id >= total_warps) return; int t = global_warp_id % seq_len; int tmp = global_warp_id / seq_len; int h = tmp % num_q_heads; int b = tmp / num_q_heads; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int kv_head = h / group_size; // Load q for this position float q_val = 0.0f; if (lane_id < head_dim) { q_val = q[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane_id]; } // Online softmax float m = -1e30f; float l = 0.0f; float o_acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= t; ++j) { // Compute score = dot(q, k_j) float k_val = 0.0f; if (lane_id < head_dim) { k_val = k[((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim + lane_id]; } float partial = (lane_id < head_dim) ? q_val * k_val : 0.0f; float s = warp_reduce_sum(partial) * scale; float m_new = fmaxf(m, s); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_s = expf(s - m_new); // Update output accumulator float v_val = 0.0f; if (lane_id < head_dim) { v_val = v[((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim + lane_id]; } o_acc = o_acc * exp_m_diff + v_val * exp_s; l = l * exp_m_diff + exp_s; m = m_new; } // Normalize if (lane_id < head_dim) { out[((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane_id] = o_acc / l; } } ``` This should have much better memory coalescing since all 32 threads in a warp read consecutive dimensions d of the same (b,j,h) vector, resulting in coalesced global memory access. And the dot product is computed via warp shuffle rather than putting everything in one thread. Let me implement this. I need to make sure threads don't diverge on `lane_id < head_dim` - since head_dim = 32 in this case, all 32 threads participate, so no divergence! Total positions = 2 * 8 * 80 = 1280. HEAD_DIM doesn't matter for the reduction. Let me write the kernel cleanly. Actually, I realize I can make it even more efficient by having each block process multiple positions. Actually, one more thought: since the problem says batch_size=2, seq_len=80, num_q_heads=8, num_kv_heads=2, head_dim=32, maybe I should consider a completely different approach like having each block handle a whole (batch, kv_head) and process all related q_heads. For a (batch, kv_head) pair, there are group_size=4 query heads and seq_len=80 positions per query head. Total of 320 positions. If a block handles all positions for a single (b, kv_head), it would process 320 positions. But with 256 threads, we could have each thread handle 1.25 positions... not clean. Alternatively, we could load the entire K and V history for a kv_head into shared memory (80*32*4 = 10KB for both K and V), and then all threads computing query positions for that kv_head could read from shared memory. This would be: - Block processes one (b, kv_head) - Threads are organized as (q_head_in_group, t, d) where q_head_in_group in [0,4), t in [0,80), d in [0,7) or something. With 4*80 = 320 positions and 32 dims, if each thread handles one dimension of one position, we'd need 320*32 = 10240 threads. That's too many. What if each block handles one (b, q_head) - 80 positions? We could have each warp handle one position (t), with 80 warps = 2560 threads. Still too many. What if each block handles 16 positions (say 2 warps per position, 16/8 = wait...) Let me think differently. blockDim = 256 (8 warps). Each warp handles 1 (b, h, t). - 1 block handles 8 positions - To handle 1280 positions, we need 160 blocks K/V reads: each warp loads 32 floats (128 bytes) for k and 32 floats for v per iteration. At j=80, that's 80*256 bytes = 20KB of reads per block. With 160 blocks total * 20KB = 3.2MB. This is tiny. The memory bandwidth of H100 is ~3-4 TB/s. For 3.2MB, the theoretical minimum time is about 1 microsecond. But we're doing this sequentially in the inner loop, so it might take longer. Wait, 160 blocks may not all run concurrently. Let's think about SM occupancy. H100 has 132 SMs. If each block uses 256 threads = 2 thread blocks per SM (max 2048 threads per SM). So about 264 blocks can run simultaneously. We only have 160, so we can saturate all SMs. Actually, each SM can hold 2 blocks of 256 threads, so total concurrent blocks = 264. Our 160 blocks fit. Each block takes some time to run. Since memory reads are coalesced now, each warp's coalesced read of 128 bytes takes ~1 cycle at L2 bandwidth. For 80 iterations * 2 reads (k and v) * 1 cycle = ~160 cycles. Plus math. At 2 GHz, 160 cycles = 80 nanoseconds per warp. For 8 warps per block, that's negligible since they run in parallel within the SM. Wait, but each block only has 8 warps! All 8 warps run on the same SM and share memory bandwidth. So the block's execution time is determined by the slowest warp, which is roughly the time for 80 iterations. Actually all warps in a block run concurrently on the same SM (interleaved). Memory bandwidth per SM is ~25-30 GB/s (total ~3 TB/s across 132 SMs). 128 bytes per warp * 8 warps * 80 iterations * 2 (k and v) = 163,840 bytes per block. At 25 GB/s, that's 6.5 microseconds. Hmm, in my first naive kernel I had 1280 threads, each doing uncoalesced reads. That was much worse because each thread reads 80 kv positions * 2 * 32 floats = 5120 floats = 20KB. Total reads = 1280 * 20KB = 25MB. With uncoalesced reads, effective bandwidth is terrible. In the new kernel with coalesced reads: total reads = 1280 positions * 80 kv positions * 2 (k,v) * 32 dims * 4 bytes = 26,214,400 bytes = 26MB. This is roughly the same volume, but now it's read in a coalesced manner => much higher effective bandwidth. Wait, but actually both kernels read the same amount of data. The improvement is purely from coalescing. At 3TB/s HBM bandwidth, 26MB takes ~8.7 microseconds. So perhaps the kernel should be roughly 10-20 microseconds, which is probably faster than the reference. Let me just compile and test. I'll add a few more optimizations: 1. Load q into shared memory or register (register is fine) 2. Use online softmax for numerical stability 3. Use __ldg for read-only data 4. Consider using float4 loads when head_dim=32 (8 float4 per warp) Actually for head_dim=32, each warp loads 32 floats = 8 float4's when aligned. We could use float4 to further reduce instruction count. But we must ensure alignment: head_dim * sizeof(float) = 128, and the tensor stride between heads may not be aligned to 16 bytes. Let me check: q shape is (2, 80, 8, 32). The stride from dim=-1 perspective: to go from q[b,t,h,d] to q[b,t,h,d+4], we stride by 1. To go from h=0 to h=1, we stride by 32 floats = 128 bytes = 16 * 8. This is 16-byte aligned. To go from t=0 to t=1, we stride by 8*32 = 256 floats = 1024 bytes, also 16-byte aligned. So float4 loads should be safe for contiguous tensors! Let me use float4 for loading q, k, v. This will halve the number of load instructions. With head_dim=32, each position has 8 float4 elements. For the warp kernel: - Each thread handles 1 float (not float4). Or we can have each thread handle 1 float4 = 4 floats. But then we'd only need 8 threads per warp for head_dim=32, and the other 24 would be idle. Alternatively, we can have 8 threads active (divided into 2 sub-warps or just let 24 threads be idle). But warp divergence would be bad because __shfl_down_sync would need all threads to participate. What if we have each thread process 4 dimensions? Then 8 threads would compute the partial products. We'd need to reduce across 8 threads. __shfl_down_sync works with 8 threads too if we use proper masks. Actually, the simplest is to have all 32 threads handle 1 float each. For head_dim=32, threads 0-31 each handle one dimension. For the warp reduction, we reduce all 32 partials. The memory coalescing is perfect: thread 0 reads element 0, thread 1 reads element 1, ..., thread 31 reads element 31. That's a perfect 128-byte coalesced read. Let me just use simple float reads and trust the compiler to handle them efficiently. Actually, let me add a template for head_dim and use static unrolling to help the compiler. But the dispatch approach with the extra host code is annoying. I'll keep it simple with runtime head_dim but add `pragma unroll` for the dimension loop. Wait, if head_dim is not known at compile time, `pragma unroll` won't help. For head_dim=32, I can use a switch statement but that requires significant boilerplate. Let me just write the kernel without templates and rely on the fact that 32 is small enough that the loop overhead is negligible. Actually, the loop overhead for 32 iterations is tiny compared to the memory access. Let me not worry about unrolling. Let me write and compile. I'll keep block_size at 256 (8 warps per block). Since we need to check correctness first, let me run that. One more optimization: since all threads in a warp read k and v from the same (b, j, kv_head) but different dimensions, their accesses are perfectly coalesced across the warp. And since we're doing this for j=0 to t sequentially, the L1 cache will likely retain some of these values, especially since different warps in the same block may be reading the same j values for the same (b, kv_head). Wait, different warps in a block might be handling different (b, h, t). Two warps in the same block could share the same b and kv_head but different h or t. For a given j, they all read k[b, j, kv_head, :] which is the same data! This means if multiple warps in the same block read the same k/v vector, they can benefit from L1 cache hits! How many warps in a block share the same b and kv_head? Each block has 8 warps. The warps are assigned by global_warp_id = blockIdx.x * 8 + warp_id. global_warp_id = b * num_q_heads * seq_len + h * seq_len + t. So warps in the same block have consecutive global_warp_id's. For block 0: warps 0-7 correspond to: - (b=0, h=0, t=0,1,2,3,4,5,6,7) All have b=0, kv_head=0 (since h=0, group_size=4, kv_head=0/4=0). So all 8 warps in block 0 share the same b=0 and kv_head=0! That's great for L1 cache. For block 1: warps 8-15: (b=0, h=0, t=8..15) - same b, same kv_head! ... For block 9: warps 72-79: (b=0, h=0, t=72..79) For block 10: warps 80-87: (b=0, h=0's last position? Wait, 80 positions per head) Actually seq_len=80, so h=0 has positions t=0..79. That's 10 blocks if each block does 8 positions. Then block 10 would be warp 80: (b=0, h=1, t=0) with kv_head = 1/4 = 0. All warps in blocks 10-19 have b=0, kv_head=0. ... Blocks 20-29: b=0, h=2, kv_head=0. Blocks 30-39: b=0, h=3, kv_head=0. Blocks 40-49: b=0, h=4, kv_head=1. ... So within a block, all warps share the same b, h, and thus kv_head! The only difference is t. This means for a given j, all warps in the block read the same k[b,j,kv_head,:] and v[b,j,kv_head,:]! Yes! This is excellent for cache locality. All 8 warps in a block access the same K and V vectors at each step j, so the L1 cache will have massive hit rates. The first warp to read j will load it into L1, and the other 7 warps will get cache hits. This is a huge optimization! Let me write the kernel. Also note: since head_dim=32 and we're doing coalesced reads of 128 bytes, the entire K/V vector fits in 1 cache line. So all 8 warps in the block hit the same cache line. The kernel should be very efficient. Let me write it now. One more micro-optimization: I can load q_val once into a register and reuse it for all j. That's done. For the softmax: - Each warp computes its own s_j - m and l are per-warp (not shared across warps) - final output is per-warp So there's no need for inter-warp communication! Each warp is completely independent. Great, let me write the kernel. ```python import os import sys import torch import torch.nn as nn conda_lib = os.path.join(os.path.dirname(sys.executable), '..', 'lib') conda_lib = os.path.normpath(conda_lib) if os.path.isdir(conda_lib): current = os.environ.get('LD_LIBRARY_PATH', '') os.environ['LD_LIBRARY_PATH'] = f"{conda_lib}:{current}" if current else conda_lib try: import ctypes ctypes.CDLL(os.path.join(conda_lib, 'libstdc++.so.6'), mode=ctypes.RTLD_GLOBAL) except: pass from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cuda_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> __device__ __forceinline__ float warp_reduce_sum(float val) { #pragma unroll for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) { val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); } return val; } __global__ void causal_gqa_forward_kernel( const float* __restrict__ q, const float* __restrict__ k, const float* __restrict__ v, float* __restrict__ out, int batch_size, int seq_len, int num_q_heads, int num_kv_heads, int head_dim, float scale) { int lane_id = threadIdx.x & 31; // threadIdx.x % 32 int warp_id = threadIdx.x >> 5; // threadIdx.x / 32 int warps_per_block = blockDim.x >> 5; int global_warp_id = blockIdx.x * warps_per_block + warp_id; int total_warps = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; if (global_warp_id >= total_warps) return; // Decode global_warp_id into (b, h, t) int t = global_warp_id % seq_len; int tmp = global_warp_id / seq_len; int h = tmp % num_q_heads; int b = tmp / num_q_heads; int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads; int kv_head = h / group_size; // Load q for this thread's dimension int q_idx = ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane_id; float q_val = (lane_id < head_dim) ? q[q_idx] : 0.0f; // Online softmax state float m = -1e30f; float l = 0.0f; float o_acc = 0.0f; for (int j = 0; j <= t; ++j) { // Compute score = dot(q, k_j) * scale int k_idx = ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim + lane_id; float k_val = (lane_id < head_dim) ? k[k_idx] : 0.0f; float partial = q_val * k_val; float s = warp_reduce_sum(partial) * scale; // Online softmax update float m_new = fmaxf(m, s); float exp_m_diff = expf(m - m_new); float exp_s = expf(s - m_new); // Update output accumulator int v_idx = ((b * seq_len + j) * num_kv_heads + kv_head) * head_dim + lane_id; float v_val = (lane_id < head_dim) ? v[v_idx] : 0.0f; o_acc = o_acc * exp_m_diff + v_val * exp_s; l = l * exp_m_diff + exp_s; m = m_new; } // Write output int out_idx = ((b * seq_len + t) * num_q_heads + h) * head_dim + lane_id; if (lane_id < head_dim) { out[out_idx] = o_acc / l; } } torch::Tensor causal_gqa_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v) { int batch_size = q.size(0); int seq_len = q.size(1); int num_q_heads = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); int num_kv_heads = k.size(2); auto out = torch::zeros_like(q); int total_warps = batch_size * num_q_heads * seq_len; const int warps_per_block = 8; // 256 threads / 32 = 8 warps int num_blocks = (total_warps + warps_per_block - 1) / warps_per_block; int block_size = warps_per_block * 32; float scale = 1.0f / sqrtf(static_cast<float>(head_dim)); causal_gqa_forward_kernel<<<num_blocks, block_size>>>( q.data_ptr<float>(), k.data_ptr<float>(), v.data_ptr<float>(), out.data_ptr<float>(), batch_size, seq_len, num_q_heads, num_kv_heads, head_dim, scale); cudaError_t err = cudaGetLastError(); if (err != cudaSuccess) { TORCH_CHECK(false, "CUDA kernel failed: ", cudaGetErrorString(err)); } return out; } """ cpp_source = r""" #include <torch/extension.h> torch::Tensor causal_gqa_forward(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor v); """ _causal_gqa = load_inline( name='causal_gqa_v6', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['causal_gqa_forward'], extra_ldflags=['-static-libstdc++'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False, ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self): super().__init__() def forward(self, q, k, v): q = q.contiguous().float() k = k.contiguous().float() v = v.contiguous().float() return _causal_gqa.causal_gqa_forward(q, k, v) ```

reasoning
The correctness check failed. The issue is likely that the reference PyTorch code uses double-precision for some computations (like math.sqrt and sum operations in PyTorch default to higher precision / are more accurate). The differences (max ~4.2) might be due to numerical differences in the softmax computation, or because the reference does the computation with higher precision or different reduction order. Wait, but the first kernel (simple sequential) passed! So the issue is in the warp reduction kernel. In the simple sequential kernel, each thread loads ALL head_dim elements, computes the dot product sequentially (same order as reference), and does softmax. That worked. Let me trace it for 4 threads: Initial: t0=a, t1=b, t2=c, t3=d offset=2 (for warpSize=4, we start with offset=2): t0 gets t2 -> a+c t1 gets t3 -> b+d t2 gets t4 (wrap/self) -> c t3 gets t5 (wrap/self) -> d offset=1: t0 gets t1 -> (a+c)+(b+d) = a+b+c+d t1 gets t2 (which is c) -> (b+d)+c t2 gets t3 (which is d) -> c+d t3 gets t4 (self) -> d So only lane 0 has the correct sum! The other lanes have incorrect partial sums. To fix this, we should use `__shfl_xor_sync` instead! ```cpp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_xor_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); ``` For XOR shuffle: offset=2: t0 gets t2 (0^2=2) -> a+c t1 gets t3 (1^2=3) -> b+d t2 gets t0 (2^2=0) -> c+a t3 gets t1 (3^2=1) -> d+b offset=1: t0 gets t1 (0^1=1) -> (a+c)+(b+d) t1 gets t0 (1^1=0) -> (b+d)+(a+c) t2 gets t3 (2^1=3) -> (a+c)+(b+d) t3 gets t2 (3^1=2) -> (b+d)+(a+c) Now all lanes have the correct sum! Alternatively, with `__shfl_down_sync`, we need to broadcast the result from lane 0 after the reduction. ```cpp for (int offset = 16; offset > 0; offset >>= 1) val += __shfl_down_sync(0xffffffff, val, offset); val = __shfl_sync(0xffffffff, val, 0); ``` So the bug was that `s` was only correct in lane 0, not in all lanes! Since I didn't broadcast `s` to all lanes, lanes 1-31 were using their own incorrect partial sums for the softmax, leading to completely wrong results. Let me fix this by either using `__shfl_xor_sync` or broadcasting the result. Using `__shfl_xor_sync` is cleaner. If the test has head_dim=32 (which it does according to the problem statement), that should be fine. But I should test it. Wait, actually I compiled v9, let me run correctness on it. Then there's another optimization to try - instead of complete inline unrolling for head_dim=32, maybe I should use template specialization with a loop that the compiler can unroll better. Actually, let me think about what went wrong with v9. The v9 kernel uses a loop structure similar to v5 but with aggressive manual unrolling. If v5 is correct, v9 should be correct too, since it just unrolls the loops for head_dim=32. But wait, v9 has a TORCH_CHECK(head_dim <= 32), but head_dim is 32, so it should pass. Let me test correctness first. Then I can think about further optimizations: for each thread we process one (b,h,t). The seq_len is only 80, so the max t is 79. The loop does 80 iterations max. But with 8 warps per block and 1280 total warps (2*8*80 = 1280), using 256 threads per block: Actually, let me think about performance more carefully. The problem is small: batch_size=2, seq_len=80, num_q_heads=8, head_dim=32. Total positions (b, h, t) = 2*8*80 = 1280. My v7 uses one warp per (b, h, t), with 32 threads doing a reduction. So I have 1280 warps. With 8 warps per block = 160 blocks. For warp reduction, within each warp: - Each thread reads one q value (if lane_id < head_dim=32) - For each j from 0 to t (max 80): - Each thread reads one k value and computes q*k - Warp reduce sum (via __shfl_xor_sync) - this is fast, O(log(32)) ≈ 5 shuffles + adds - Scale - Each thread reads one v value - Update m, l, o_acc - Write back For __shfl_xor_sync, all threads in the warp must participate in the shuffle. The warp is 32-wide, head_dim is 32, so all 32 lanes participate with valid values. This is correct. For v6 with __shfl_down_sync, the issue was that thread 0 gets the sum but other threads get partial results. The shuffle only propagates to threads with higher index. Since all 32 threads are doing the reduction but thread 0 gets the full sum while thread 31 only gets the partial sum from lanes 16+17... wait, no. __shfl_down_sync(val, offset) means thread i gets the value from thread i+offset. After the loop: - offset=16: threads 0-15 get val from threads 16-31 - offset=8: threads 0-7 get val from threads 8-15 - ...and thread 0 also keeps accumulating But for the reduction, typically we'd want a broadcast to thread 0, or all threads to have the same value. With __shfl_down_sync, by the end: - After offset=16: lane 0 has val0+val16, lane 1 has val1+val17, lane 2 has val2+val18, etc. - After offset=8: lane 0 has val0+val16+val8+val24, lane 1 has val1+val17+val9+val25, etc. - ... - After offset=1: lane 0 has sum of all even lanes, lane 1 has sum of all odd lanes, etc. Wait, that's not right either. Let me think again... Actually __shfl_down_sync(mask, val, offset, width=32) gives: lane i receives value from lane min(i+offset, i) For offset=16: lanes 0-15 receive from 16-31, lanes 16-31 receive from themselves (broadcast) For offset=8: lanes 0-7 receive from 8-15, lanes 8-15 receive from 16-23, etc. In the code: float s = warp_reduce_sum(partial); where warp_reduce_sum is: val += __shfl_down_sync(0xFFFFFFFF, val, offset); After each iteration, lane i += lane(i+offset)'s value (for i < 16 after first iter). After offset=16: lane0 += lane16, lane1 += lane17, ... lane15 += lane31 After offset=8: lane0 += lane8, lane1 += lane9, ... lane7 += lane15 After offset=4: lane0 += lane4, lane1 += lane5, lane2 += lane6, lane3 += lane7 After offset=2: lane0 += lane2, lane1 += lane3 After offset=1: lane0 += lane1 So lane0 ends up with the full sum, but lane1 only has lane1+lane3+lane5+lane7+... (not all lanes). And in the code, each thread uses its own `s` value individually: float s = warp_reduce_sum(partial) * scale; So thread0 gets the correct score but thread1 gets only a partial sum! That's why v6 was wrong. Using __shfl_xor_sync fixes this because: lane i += lane(i^offset) So after offset=16: lane0 += lane16, lane1 += lane17, etc. After offset=8: lane0 += lane8, lane1 += lane9, etc. After all iterations, every lane ends up with the full sum because XOR distributes evenly. Kernel outline: For each block = (b, kv_head): - Load k[b, :, kv_head, :] and v[b, :, kv_head, :] into shared memory - Then each warp/thread processes the q-heads in that group Decoding: blockIdx.x maps to (b, kv_head) pair: pair_id = blockIdx.x b = pair_id / num_kv_heads kv_head = pair_id % num_kv_heads q-heads for this kv_head: h_start = kv_head * group_size, h_end = h_start + group_size Each thread in the block processes some (h, t) positions. With group_size=4, there are 4*80=320 positions. With blockDim.x=128: each thread handles 320/128 = 2.5 positions → some handle 2, some handle 3. But that gets complicated. Simpler: each thread processes one (h, t) and computes dot products serially using smem_k and smem_v. With head_dim=32, each dot product is 32 multiplies and 31 adds. For t=80, that's 80 dot products = ~2560 multiplies per output. Each thread handles ~3 outputs → ~7680 multiplies. On a GPU, this is very small. Actually, I think the key bottleneck is not compute but memory latency and instruction overhead. The unrolled version (v9) should help by reducing loop overhead. For the shared memory version, I'd need to carefully manage loads and avoid bank conflicts. For head_dim=32, loading k into shared memory with a coalesced pattern: - Thread i loads k[b, s, kv_head, i] for some s - The stride between threads is 1 float (4 bytes), so this is coalesced But then when computing dot products, each thread reads shared memory at different j and d. If we store k in shared memory as [seq_len][head_dim], then thread accesses are strided. Actually, let's look at the access pattern. If a thread is computing dot(q[t], k[j]) for j=0..t, it accesses k[0][0..31], k[1][0..31], ..., k[t][0..31]. This is sequential in j, and for each j, it accesses all d. The shared memory bank size is 4 bytes (1 float), and there are 32 banks. Accessing k[j][d] and k[j][d+1] in consecutive threads would have bank conflicts if threads are accessing the same j but different d. For a warp of 32 threads all computing dot products for different t values but the same j: - Thread 0 computes q[t0,d]*k[j,d] for d=0..31 - Thread 1 computes q[t1,d]*k[j,d] for d=0..31 - ... Each thread reads k[j,0], k[j,1], ..., k[j,31] sequentially. These are in banks 0,1,2,...31. Since it's a single thread reading sequentially, there's no bank conflict. Bank conflicts only happen when multiple threads in the same warp access the same bank. So if each thread reads its own sequential pattern, there's no conflict as long as the starting addresses map to different banks... wait, for the same j, k[j,0] is in bank 0, k[j,1] in bank 1, etc. If thread 0 reads k[j,0] and thread 1 also reads k[j,0] (for a different purpose), that's a bank conflict. But in this case, each thread reads k[j, d] for its own d. If all threads in a warp are processing the same j but different t, they each read the full k[j, 0..31] sequentially. So at any given cycle, some threads might be reading k[j,d] where d differs... this shouldn't cause bank conflicts because bank = address % 32, and since each thread is reading a different d, they go to different banks. If all 32 threads in a warp read k[j, d] for the same d at the same time, that would be a broadcast (all threads read bank d simultaneously). This doesn't cause a bank conflict on modern GPUs because broadcast is handled specially. So shared memory for k and v should be quite efficient. * seq_len;\n\n if (global_warp_id >= total_warps) return;\n\n int t = global_warp_id % seq_len;\n int tmp = global_warp_id / seq_len;\n int h = tmp % num_q_heads;\n int b = tmp / num_q_heads;\n\n int group_size = num_q_heads / num_kv_heads;\n int kv_head = h / group_size;\n\n // Load q for this thread's dimension using compile-time HEAD_DIM\n float q_val = 0.0f;\n if (lane_id < HEAD_DIM) {\n int
